href="/wiki/Template:Wpspace" title="Template:Wpspace"><span title="View this template">view</span></a></li><li class="nv-talk"><a href="/wiki/Template_talk:Wpspace" title="Template talk:Wpspace"><span title="Discuss this template">talk</span></a></li><li class="nv-edit"><a class="external text" href="//en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Template:Wpspace&amp;action=edit"><span title="Edit this template">edit</span></a></li></ul></div>\n</td></tr></tbody></table>\n<p><b>Interstellar travel</b> is the term used for <a href="/wiki/Human_spaceflight" title="Human spaceflight">crewed</a> or uncrewed travel between <a href="/wiki/Star" title="Star">stars</a> or <a href="/wiki/Planetary_system" title="Planetary system">planetary systems</a>. Interstellar travel will be much more difficult than <a href="/wiki/Interplanetary_spaceflight" title="Interplanetary spaceflight">interplanetary spaceflight</a>; the distances between the <a href="/wiki/Planet" title="Planet">planets</a> in the <a href="/wiki/Solar_System" title="Solar System">Solar System</a> are less than 30 <a href="/wiki/Astronomical_unit" title="Astronomical unit">astronomical units</a> (AU)&#8212;whereas the distances between stars are typically hundreds of thousands of AU, and usually expressed in <a href="/wiki/Light-year" title="Light-year">light-years</a>. Because of the vastness of those distances, interstellar travel would require a high percentage of the <a href="/wiki/Speed_of_light" title="Speed of light">speed of light</a>; huge travel time, lasting from decades to millennia or longer; or a combination of both.\n</p><p>The speeds required for interstellar travel in a human lifetime far exceed what current methods of <a href="/wiki/Spacecraft_propulsion" title="Spacecraft propulsion">spacecraft propulsion</a> can provide. Even with a hypothetically perfectly efficient propulsion system, the <a href="/wiki/Kinetic_energy" title="Kinetic energy">kinetic energy</a> corresponding to those speeds is enormous by today\'s standards of <a href="/wiki/Energy_development" title="Energy development">energy development</a>. Moreover, collisions by the spacecraft with <a href="/wiki/Cosmic_dust" title="Cosmic dust">cosmic dust</a> and gas can produce very dangerous effects both to passengers and the spacecraft itself.\n</p><p>A number of strategies have been proposed to deal with these problems, ranging from giant arks that would carry entire societies and ecosystems, to microscopic <a href="/wiki/Space_probe" title="Space probe">space probes</a>. Many different <a href="/wiki/Spacecraft_propulsion" title="Spacecraft propulsion">spacecraft propulsion</a> systems have been proposed to give spacecraft the required speeds, including <a href="/wiki/Nuclear_propulsion" title="Nuclear propulsion">nuclear propulsion</a>, <a href="/wiki/Beam-powered_propulsion" title="Beam-powered propulsion">beam-powered propulsion</a>, and methods based on speculative physics.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-1"><a href="#cite_note-1">[1]</a></sup>\n</p><p>For both crewed and uncrewed interstellar travel, considerable technological and economic challenges need to be met. Even the most optimistic views about interstellar travel see it as only being feasible decades from now. However, in spite of the challenges, if or when interstellar travel is realised, a wide range of scientific benefits is expected.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-2"><a href="#cite_note-2">[2]</a></sup>\n</p><p>Most interstellar travel concepts require a developed space logistics system capable of moving millions of tons to a construction / operating location, and most would require gigawatt-scale power for construction or power (such as <a href="/wiki/Starwisp" title="Starwisp">Star Wisp</a> or <a href="/wiki/Solar_sail" title="Solar sail">Light Sail</a> type concepts). Such a system could grow organically if <a href="/wiki/Space-based_solar_power" title="Space-based solar power">space-based solar power</a> became a significant component of Earth\'s energy mix. Consumer demand for a multi-terawatt system would automatically create the necessary multi-million ton/year logistical system.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-3"><a href="#cite_note-3">[3]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r872693506">.mw-parser-output .toclimit-2 .toclevel-1 ul,.mw-parser-output .toclimit-3 .toclevel-2 class="tocnumber">5.2</span> <span class="toctext">Project Hyperion</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-43"><a href="#NASA_research"><span class="tocnumber">5.3</span> <span class="toctext">NASA research</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-44"><a href="#100_Year_Starship_study"><span class="tocnumber">5.4</span> <span class="toctext">100 Year Starship study</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-45"><a href="#Other_designs"><span class="tocnumber">5.5</span> <span class="toctext">Other designs</span></a></li>\n</ul>\n</li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-46"><a href="#Non-profit_organizations"><span class="tocnumber">6</span> <span class="toctext">Non-profit organizations</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-47"><a href="#Feasibility"><span class="tocnumber">7</span> <span class="toctext">Feasibility</span></a>\n<ul>\n<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-48"><a href="#Interstellar_missions_not_for_human_benefit"><span class="tocnumber">7.1</span> <span class="toctext">Interstellar missions not for human benefit</span></a></li>\n</ul>\n</li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-49"><a href="#Discovery_of_Earth-Like_planets"><span class="tocnumber">8</span> <span class="toctext">Discovery of Earth-Like planets</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-50"><a href="#See_also"><span class="tocnumber">9</span> <span class="toctext">See also</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-51"><a href="#References"><span class="tocnumber">10</span> <span class="toctext">References</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-52"><a href="#Further_reading"><span class="tocnumber">11</span> <span class="toctext">Further reading</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-53"><a href="#External_links"><span class="tocnumber">12</span> <span class="toctext">External links</span></a></li>\n</ul>\n</div>\n</div>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Challenges">Challenges</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Interstellar_travel&amp;action=edit&amp;section=1" title="Edit section: Challenges">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Interstellar_distances">Interstellar distances</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Interstellar_travel&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: Interstellar distances">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<p>Distances between the planets in the Solar System are often measured in astronomical units (AU), defined as the average distance between the Sun and Earth, some 1.5<span style="margin:0 .15em 0 .25em">&#215;</span>10<sup>8</sup> kilometers (93&#160;million miles). <a href="/wiki/Venus" title="Venus">Venus</a>, the closest other planet to Earth is (at closest approach) 0.28 AU away. <a href="/wiki/Neptune" title="Neptune">Neptune</a>, the farthest planet from the Sun, is 29.8 AU away. As of January 2018, <a href="/wiki/Voyager_1" title="Voyager 1">Voyager&#160;1</a>, the farthest man-made object from Earth, is 141.5 AU away.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-voyager_4-0"><a href="#cite_note-voyager-4">[4]</a></sup>\n</p><p>The closest known star, <a href="/wiki/Proxima_Centauri" title="Proxima Centauri">Proxima Centauri</a>, is approximately 268,332&#160;AU away, or over 9,000 times farther away than Neptune.\n</p>\n<table class="wikitable" style="margin: 1em auto 1em auto;">\n<tbody><tr>\n<th>Object\n</th>\n<th>A.U.\n</th>\n<th>light time\n</th></tr>\n<tr>\n<td><a href="/wiki/Moon" title="Moon">Moon</a>\n</td>\n<td>0.0026\n</td>\n<td>1.3 seconds\n</td></tr>\n<tr>\n<td><a href="/wiki/Sun" title="Sun">Sun</a>\n</td>\n<td>1\n</td>\n<td>8 minutes\n</td></tr>\n<tr>\n<td><a href="/wiki/Venus" title="Venus">Venus</a> (nearest planet)\n</td>\n<td>0.28\n</td>\n<td>2.41 minutes\n</td></tr>\n<tr>\n<td><a href="/wiki/Neptune" title="Neptune">Neptune</a> (farthest planet)\n</td>\n<td>29.8\n</td>\n<td>4.1 hours\n</td></tr>\n<tr>\n<td><a href="/wiki/Voyager_1" title="Voyager 1">Voyager 1</a>\n</td>\n<td>141.5\n</td>\n<td>19.61 hours\n</td></tr>\n<tr>\n<td><a href="/wiki/Proxima_Centauri" title="Proxima Centauri">Proxima Centauri</a> (nearest star and exoplanet)&#160; &#160;\n</td>\n<td>268,332&#160;\n</td>\n<td>4.24 years\n</td></tr></tbody></table>\n<p>Because of this, distances between stars are usually expressed in <a href="/wiki/Light-year" title="Light-year">light-years</a>, defined as the distance that a <a href="/wiki/Light" title="Light">light</a> <a href="/wiki/Photon" title="Photon">photon</a> travels in a year. Light in a vacuum travels around 300,000 kilometres (186,000&#160;mi) per second, so this is some 9.461<span style="margin:0 .15em 0 .25em">&#215;</span>10<sup>12</sup> kilometers (5.879&#160;trillion miles) or 1 light-year (63,241&#160;AU) in a year. Proxima Centauri is 4.243 light-years away.\n</p><p>Another way of understanding the vastness of interstellar distances is by scaling: One of the closest stars to the Sun, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Alpha_Centauri_A" title="Alpha Centauri A">Alpha Centauri A</a> (a Sun-like star), can be pictured by scaling down the <a href="/wiki/Astronomical_unit" title="Astronomical unit">Earth&#8211;Sun distance</a> to one meter (3.28&#160;ft). On this scale, the distance to Alpha Centauri A would be 276 kilometers (171 miles).\n</p><p>The fastest outward-bound spacecraft yet sent, <a href="/wiki/Voyager_1#Interstellar_mission" title="Voyager 1">Voyager 1</a>, has covered 1/600 of a light-year in 30 years and is currently moving at 1/18,000 the speed of light. At this rate, a journey to Proxima Centauri would take 80,000 years.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-5"><a href="#cite_note-5">[5]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Required_energy">Required energy</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Interstellar_travel&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: Required energy">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<p>A significant factor contributing to the difficulty is the energy that must be supplied to obtain a reasonable travel time. A lower bound for the required energy is the <a href="/wiki/Kinetic_energy" title="Kinetic energy">kinetic energy</a> <span class="mwe-math-element"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math alttext="{\\displaystyle K={\\tfrac {1}{2}}mv^{2}}" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">\n<semantics>\n<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n<mstyle displaystyle="true" scriptlevel="0">\n<mi>K</mi>\n<mo>=</mo>\n<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n<mstyle displaystyle="false" scriptlevel="0">\n<mfrac>\n<mn>1</mn>\n<mn>2</mn>\n</mfrac>\n</mstyle>\n</mrow>\n<mi>m</mi>\n<msup>\n<mi>v</mi>\n<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n<mn>2</mn>\n</mrow>\n</msup>\n</mstyle>\n</mrow>\n<annotation encoding="application/x-tex">{\\displaystyle K={\\tfrac {1}{2}}mv^{2}}</annotation>\n</semantics>\n</math></span><img alt="{\\displaystyle K={\\tfrac {1}{2}}mv^{2}}" aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/9e76c32a8892c3935122d877492dd9d06a58e9b2" style="vertical-align: -1.171ex; width:11.045ex; height:3.509ex;"/></span> where <span class="mwe-math-element"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math alttext="{\\displaystyle m}" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">\n<semantics>\n<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n<mstyle displaystyle="true" scriptlevel="0">\n<mi>m</mi>\n</mstyle>\n</mrow>\n<annotation encoding="application/x-tex">{\\displaystyle m}</annotation>\n</semantics>\n</math></span><img alt="m" aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/0a07d98bb302f3856cbabc47b2b9016692e3f7bc" style="vertical-align: -0.338ex; width:2.04ex; height:1.676ex;"/></span> is the final mass. If <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Deceleration" title="Deceleration">deceleration</a> on arrival is desired and cannot be achieved by any means other than the engines of the ship, then the lower bound for the required energy is doubled to <span class="mwe-math-element"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math alttext="{\\displaystyle mv^{2}}" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">\n<semantics>\n<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n<mstyle displaystyle="true" scriptlevel="0">\n<mi>m</mi>\n<msup>\n<mi>v</mi>\n<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n<mn>2</mn>\n</mrow>\n</msup>\n</mstyle>\n</mrow>\n<annotation encoding="application/x-tex">{\\displaystyle mv^{2}}</annotation>\n</semantics>\n</math></span><img alt="mv^2" aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/3c1745212e8eaaec5ba2bf755e4b673677a371c8" style="vertical-align: -0.338ex; width:4.222ex; height:2.676ex;"/></span>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-6"><a href="#cite_note-6">[6]</a></sup>\n</p><p>The velocity for a manned round trip of a few decades to even the nearest star is several thousand times greater than those of present space vehicles. This means that due to the <span class="mwe-math-element"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math alttext="{\\displaystyle v^{2}}" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">\n<semantics>\n<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n<mstyle displaystyle="true" scriptlevel="0">\n<msup>\n<mi>v</mi>\n<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n<mn>2</mn>\n</mrow>\n</msup>\n</mstyle>\n</mrow>\n<annotation encoding="application/x-tex">{\\displaystyle v^{2}}</annotation>\n</semantics>\n</math></span><img alt="v^{2}" aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/4faa98a21ac8133ab466999288849492be28b3d1" style="vertical-align: -0.338ex; width:2.182ex; height:2.676ex;"/></span> term in the kinetic energy formula, millions of times as much energy is required. Accelerating one ton to one-tenth of the speed of light requires at least 450 petajoules or 4.50<span style="margin:0 .15em 0 .25em">&#215;</span>10<sup>17</sup> joules or 125 terawatt-hours<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-7"><a href="#cite_note-7">[7]</a></sup> (<a href="/wiki/World_energy_consumption" title="World energy consumption">world energy consumption</a> 2008 was 143,851&#160;terawatt-hours),<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (July 2015)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup> without factoring in efficiency of the propulsion mechanism. This energy has to be generated onboard from stored fuel, harvested from the interstellar medium, or projected over immense distances.\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Interstellar_medium">Interstellar medium</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Interstellar_travel&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: Interstellar medium">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<p>A knowledge of the properties of the <a href="/wiki/Interstellar_medium" title="Interstellar medium">interstellar gas and dust</a> through which the vehicle must pass is essential for the design of any interstellar space mission.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ism_8-0"><a href="#cite_note-ism-8">[8]</a></sup> A major issue with traveling at extremely high speeds is that interstellar dust may cause considerable damage to the craft, due to the high relative speeds and large kinetic energies involved. Various shielding methods to mitigate this problem have been proposed.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NASA_9-0"><a href="#cite_note-NASA-9">[9]</a></sup> Larger objects (such as macroscopic dust grains) are far less common, but would be much more destructive. The risks of impacting such objects, and methods of mitigating these risks, have been discussed in the literature, but many unknowns remain<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-HGarrett_10-0"><a href="#cite_note-HGarrett-10">[10]</a></sup> and, owing to the inhomogeneous distribution of interstellar matter around the Sun, will depend on direction travelled.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ism_8-1"><a href="#cite_note-ism-8">[8]</a></sup> Although a high density interstellar medium may cause difficulties for many interstellar travel concepts, <a href="/wiki/Bussard_ramjet" title="Bussard ramjet">interstellar ramjets</a>, and some proposed concepts for decelerating interstellar spacecraft, would actually benefit from a denser interstellar medium.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ism_8-2"><a href="#cite_note-ism-8">[8]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Hazards">Hazards</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Interstellar_travel&amp;action=edit&amp;section=5" title="Edit section: Hazards">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<p>The crew of an interstellar ship would face several significant hazards, including the psychological effects of long-term <a href="/wiki/Solitude" title="Solitude">isolation</a>, the effects of exposure to <a href="/wiki/Ionizing_radiation" title="Ionizing radiation">ionizing radiation</a>, and the physiological effects of <a href="/wiki/Weightlessness" title="Weightlessness">weightlessness</a> to the muscles, joints, bones, immune system, and eyes. There also exists the risk of impact by <a href="/wiki/Micrometeoroid" title="Micrometeoroid">micrometeoroids</a> and other <a href="/wiki/Space_debris" title="Space debris">space debris</a>. These risks represent challenges that have yet to be overcome.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-11"><a href="#cite_note-11">[11]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Wait_calculation">Wait calculation</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Interstellar_travel&amp;action=edit&amp;section=6" title="Edit section: Wait calculation">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<p>It has been argued that an interstellar mission that cannot be completed within 50 years should not be started at all. Instead, assuming that a civilization is still on an increasing curve of propulsion system velocity and not yet having reached the limit, the resources should be invested in designing a better propulsion system. This is because a slow spacecraft would probably be passed by another mission sent later with more advanced propulsion (the incessant obsolescence postulate).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Bob_Forward_1996_12-0"><a href="#cite_note-Bob_Forward_1996-12">[12]</a></sup> On the other hand, Andrew Kennedy has shown that if one calculates the journey time to a given destination as the rate of travel speed derived from growth (even exponential growth) increases, there is a clear minimum in the total time to that destination from now.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-13"><a href="#cite_note-13">[13]</a></sup> Voyages undertaken before the minimum will be overtaken by those that leave at the minimum, whereas voyages that leave after the minimum will never overtake those that left at the minimum.\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Prime_targets_for_interstellar_travel">Prime targets for interstellar travel</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Interstellar_travel&amp;action=edit&amp;section=7" title="Edit section: Prime targets for interstellar travel">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<p>There are 59 known <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Stellar_system" title="Stellar system">stellar systems</a> within 40 light years of the Sun, containing 81 visible stars. The following could be considered prime targets for interstellar missions:<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Bob_Forward_1996_12-1"><a href="#cite_note-Bob_Forward_1996-12">[12]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<table class="wikitable" style="text-align:left">\n<tbody><tr>\n<th>System</th>\n<th>Distance (ly)</th>\n<th>Remarks\n</th></tr>\n<tr>\n<td><a href="/wiki/Alpha_Centauri" title="Alpha Centauri">Alpha&#160;Centauri</a></td>\n<td>4.3</td>\n<td>Closest system. Three stars (G2, K1, M5). Component&#160;A <a href="/wiki/Solar_analog" title="Solar analog">is similar to the Sun</a> (a G2 star). On August 24, 2016, the discovery of an Earth-size <a href="/wiki/Exoplanet" title="Exoplanet">exoplanet</a> (<a href="/wiki/Proxima_Centauri_b" title="Proxima Centauri b">Proxima Centauri b</a>) orbiting in the habitable zone of <a href="/wiki/Proxima_Centauri" title="Proxima Centauri">Proxima Centauri</a> was announced.\n</td></tr>\n<tr>\n<td><a href="/wiki/Barnard%27s_Star" title="Barnard\'s Star">Barnard\'s Star</a></td>\n<td>6</td>\n<td>Small, low-luminosity M5&#160;<a href="/wiki/Red_dwarf" title="Red dwarf">red dwarf</a>. Second closest to Solar System.\n</td></tr>\n<tr>\n<td><a href="/wiki/Sirius" title="Sirius">Sirius</a></td>\n<td>8.7</td>\n<td>Large, very bright A1&#160;star with a <a href="/wiki/White_dwarf" title="White dwarf">white dwarf</a> companion.\n</td></tr>\n<tr>\n<td><a href="/wiki/Epsilon_Eridani" title="Epsilon Eridani">Epsilon Eridani</a></td>\n<td>10.8</td>\n<td>Single K2&#160;star slightly smaller and colder than the Sun. It has two asteroid belts, might have a giant and one much smaller planet,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-14"><a href="#cite_note-14">[14]</a></sup> and may possess a Solar-System-type planetary system.\n</td></tr>\n<tr>\n<td><a href="/wiki/Tau_Ceti" title="Tau Ceti">Tau Ceti</a></td>\n<td>11.8</td>\n<td>Single <a href="/wiki/G-type_main-sequence_star" title="G-type main-sequence star">G8 star</a> similar to the Sun. High probability of possessing a Solar-System-type planetary system: current evidence shows 5 planets with potentially two in the habitable zone.\n</td></tr>\n<tr>\n<td><a href="/wiki/Wolf_1061" title="Wolf 1061">Wolf 1061</a></td>\n<td>~14</td>\n<td><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Wolf_1061_c" title="Wolf 1061 c">Wolf 1061 c</a> is 4.3 times the size of Earth; it may have rocky terrain. It also sits within the &#8216;Goldilocks&#8217; zone where it might be possible for liquid water to exist.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-15"><a href="#cite_note-15">[15]</a></sup>\n</td></tr>\n<tr>\n<td><a href="/wiki/Gliese_581_planetary_system" title="Gliese 581 planetary system">Gliese 581 planetary system</a></td>\n<td>20.3</td>\n<td>Multiple planet system. The unconfirmed exoplanet <a href="/wiki/Gliese_581g" title="Gliese 581g">Gliese 581g</a> and the confirmed exoplanet <a href="/wiki/Gliese_581d" title="Gliese 581d">Gliese 581d</a> are in the star\'s <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Habitable_zone" title="Habitable zone">habitable zone</a>.\n</td></tr>\n<tr>\n<td><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Gliese_667C" title="Gliese 667C">Gliese 667C</a></td>\n<td>22</td>\n<td>A system with at least six planets. A record-breaking three of these planets are super-Earths lying in the zone around the star where liquid water could exist, making them possible candidates for the presence of life.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-16"><a href="#cite_note-16">[16]</a></sup>\n</td></tr>\n<tr>\n<td><a href="/wiki/Vega" title="Vega">Vega</a></td>\n<td>25</td>\n<td>A very young system possibly in the process of planetary formation.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-17"><a href="#cite_note-17">[17]</a></sup>\n</td></tr>\n<tr>\n<td><a href="/wiki/TRAPPIST-1" title="TRAPPIST-1">TRAPPIST-1</a></td>\n<td>39</td>\n<td>A recently discovered system which boasts 7 Earth-like planets, some of which may have liquid water. The discovery is a major advancement in finding a habitable planet and in finding a planet that could support life.\n</td></tr></tbody></table>\n<p>Existing and near-term astronomical technology is capable of finding planetary systems around these objects, increasing their potential for exploration\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Proposed_methods">Proposed methods</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Interstellar_travel&amp;action=edit&amp;section=8" title="Edit section: Proposed methods">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<h3><span id="Slow.2C_uncrewed_probes"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Slow,_uncrewed_probes">Slow, uncrewed probes</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Interstellar_travel&amp;action=edit&amp;section=9" title="Edit section: Slow, uncrewed probes">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Interstellar_probe" title="Interstellar probe">Interstellar probe</a></div>\n<p>Slow interstellar missions based on current and near-future propulsion technologies are associated with trip times starting from about one hundred years to thousands of years. These missions consist of sending a robotic probe to a nearby star for exploration, similar to interplanetary probes such as used in the <a href="/wiki/Voyager_program" title="Voyager program">Voyager program</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-National_Aeronautics_and_Space_Administration_18-0"><a href="#cite_note-National_Aeronautics_and_Space_Administration-18">[18]</a></sup> By taking along no crew, the cost and complexity of the mission is significantly reduced although technology lifetime is still a significant issue next to obtaining a reasonable speed of travel. Proposed concepts include <a href="/wiki/Project_Daedalus" title="Project Daedalus">Project Daedalus</a>, <a href="/wiki/Project_Icarus_(interstellar)" title="Project Icarus (interstellar)">Project Icarus</a>, <a href="/wiki/Project_Dragonfly_(space_study)" title="Project Dragonfly (space study)">Project Dragonfly</a>, <a href="/wiki/Project_Longshot" title="Project Longshot">Project Longshot</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-centauri-dreams.org_19-0"><a href="#cite_note-centauri-dreams.org-19">[19]</a></sup> and more recently <a href="/wiki/Breakthrough_Starshot" title="Breakthrough Starshot">Breakthrough Starshot</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-20"><a href="#cite_note-20">[20]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span id="Fast.2C_uncrewed_probes"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Fast,_uncrewed_probes">Fast, uncrewed probes</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Interstellar_travel&amp;action=edit&amp;section=10" title="Edit section: Fast, uncrewed probes">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Interstellar_probe" title="Interstellar probe">Interstellar probe</a></div>\n<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Nanoprobes">Nanoprobes</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Interstellar_travel&amp;action=edit&amp;section=11" title="Edit section: Nanoprobes">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>\n<p>Near-lightspeed nano spacecraft might be possible within the near future built on existing microchip technology with a newly developed nanoscale thruster. Researchers at the <a href="/wiki/University_of_Michigan" title="University of Michigan">University of Michigan</a> are developing thrusters that use nanoparticles as propellant. Their technology is called "nanoparticle field extraction thruster", or <a href="/wiki/Nano-particle_field_extraction_thruster" title="Nano-particle field extraction thruster">nanoFET</a>. These devices act like small particle accelerators shooting conductive nanoparticles out into space.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-21"><a href="#cite_note-21">[21]</a></sup>\n</p><p><a href="/wiki/Michio_Kaku" title="Michio Kaku">Michio Kaku</a>, a theoretical physicist, has suggested that clouds of "smart dust" be sent to the stars, which may become possible with advances in <a href="/wiki/Nanotechnology" title="Nanotechnology">nanotechnology</a>. Kaku also notes that a large number of nanoprobes would need to be sent due to the vulnerability of very small probes to be easily deflected by magnetic fields, micrometeorites and other dangers to ensure the chances that at least one nanoprobe will survive the journey and reach the destination.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-22"><a href="#cite_note-22">[22]</a></sup>\n</p><p>Given the light weight of these probes, it would take much less energy to accelerate them. With onboard solar cells, they could continually accelerate using solar power. One can envision a day when a fleet of millions or even billions of these particles swarm to distant stars at nearly the speed of light and relay signals back to Earth through a vast interstellar communication network.\n</p><p>As a near-term solution, small, laser-propelled interstellar probes, based on current CubeSat technology were proposed in the context of <a href="/wiki/Project_Dragonfly_(space_study)" title="Project Dragonfly (space study)">Project Dragonfly</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-centauri-dreams.org_19-1"><a href="#cite_note-centauri-dreams.org-19">[19]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span id="Slow.2C_manned_missions"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Slow,_manned_missions">Slow, manned missions</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Interstellar_travel&amp;action=edit&amp;section=12" title="Edit section: Slow, manned missions">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<p>In crewed missions, the duration of a slow interstellar journey presents a major obstacle and existing concepts deal with this problem in different ways.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-23"><a href="#cite_note-23">[23]</a></sup> They can be distinguished by the "state" in which humans are transported on-board of the spacecraft.\n</p>\n<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Generation_ships">Generation ships</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Interstellar_travel&amp;action=edit&amp;section=13" title="Edit section: Generation ships">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>\n<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Generation_ship" title="Generation ship">Generation ship</a></div>\n<p>A <a href="/wiki/Generation_ship" title="Generation ship">generation ship</a> (or <b>world ship</b>) is a type of <a href="/wiki/Interstellar_ark" title="Interstellar ark">interstellar ark</a> in which the crew that arrives at the destination is descended from those who started the journey. Generation ships are not currently feasible because of the difficulty of constructing a ship of the enormous required scale and the great biological and sociological problems that life aboard such a ship raises.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Hein_2012_119&#8211;133_24-0"><a href="#cite_note-Hein_2012_119&#8211;133-24">[24]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-25"><a href="#cite_note-25">[25]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-26"><a href="#cite_note-26">[26]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-27"><a href="#cite_note-27">[27]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Suspended_animation">Suspended animation</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Interstellar_travel&amp;action=edit&amp;section=14" title="Edit section: Suspended animation">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>\n<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Sleeper_ship" title="Sleeper ship">Sleeper ship</a></div>\n<p>Scientists and writers have postulated various techniques for <a href="/wiki/Suspended_animation" title="Suspended animation">suspended animation</a>. These include human <a href="/wiki/Hibernation" title="Hibernation">hibernation</a> and <a href="/wiki/Cryonics" title="Cryonics">cryonic preservation</a>. Although neither is currently practical, they offer the possibility of <a href="/wiki/Sleeper_ship" title="Sleeper ship">sleeper ships</a> in which the passengers lie inert for the long duration of the voyage.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-28"><a href="#cite_note-28">[28]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Frozen_embryos">Frozen embryos</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Interstellar_travel&amp;action=edit&amp;section=15" title="Edit section: Frozen embryos">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>\n<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Embryo_colonization" title="Embryo colonization">Embryo colonization</a></div>\n<p>A <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Robotic" title="Robotic">robotic</a> interstellar mission carrying some number of frozen early stage human <a href="/wiki/Embryo" title="Embryo">embryos</a> is another theoretical possibility. This method of <a href="/wiki/Space_colonization" title="Space colonization">space colonization</a> requires, among other things, the development of an <a href="/wiki/Artificial_uterus" title="Artificial uterus">artificial uterus</a>, the prior detection of a habitable <a href="/wiki/Terrestrial_planet" title="Terrestrial planet">terrestrial planet</a>, and advances in the field of fully autonomous <a href="/wiki/Robotics" title="Robotics">mobile robots</a> and educational robots that would replace human parents.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-29"><a href="#cite_note-29">[29]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Island_hopping_through_interstellar_space">Island hopping through interstellar space</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Interstellar_travel&amp;action=edit&amp;section=16" title="Edit section: Island hopping through interstellar space">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<p>Interstellar space is not completely empty; it contains trillions of icy bodies ranging from small asteroids (<a href="/wiki/Oort_cloud" title="Oort cloud">Oort cloud</a>) to possible <a href="/wiki/Rogue_planet" title="Rogue planet">rogue planets</a>. There may be ways to take advantage of these resources for a good part of an interstellar trip, slowly hopping from body to body or setting up waystations along the way.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-30"><a href="#cite_note-30">[30]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Fast_missions">Fast missions</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Interstellar_travel&amp;action=edit&amp;section=17" title="Edit section: Fast missions">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<p>If a spaceship could average 10&#160;percent of light speed (and decelerate at the destination, for manned missions), this would be enough to reach <a href="/wiki/Proxima_Centauri" title="Proxima Centauri">Proxima Centauri</a> in forty years. Several propulsion concepts have been proposed <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-crawist_31-0"><a href="#cite_note-crawist-31">[31]</a></sup> that might be eventually developed to accomplish this (see <a href="/wiki/Interstellar_travel#Propulsion" title="Interstellar travel">&#167; Propulsion</a> below), but none of them are ready for near-term (few decades) developments at acceptable cost.\n</p>\n<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Time_dilation">Time dilation</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Interstellar_travel&amp;action=edit&amp;section=18" title="Edit section: Time dilation">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>\n<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Time_dilation" title="Time dilation">Time dilation</a></div>\n<p>Physicists generally believe faster-than-light travel is impossible.  \nRelativistic <a href="/wiki/Time_dilation" title="Time dilation">time dilation</a> allows a traveler to experience time more slowly, the closer his speed is to the speed of light.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-32"><a href="#cite_note-32">[32]</a></sup> This apparent slowing becomes noticeable when velocities above 80% of the speed of light are attained. Clocks aboard an interstellar ship would run slower than Earth clocks, so if a ship\'s engines were capable of continuously generating around 1&#160;g of acceleration (which is comfortable for humans), the ship could reach almost anywhere in the galaxy and return to Earth within 40 years ship-time (see diagram). Upon return, there would be a difference between the time elapsed on the astronaut\'s ship and the time elapsed on Earth.\n</p><p>For example, a spaceship could travel to a star 32 light-years away, initially accelerating at a constant 1.03g (i.e. 10.1&#160;m/s<sup>2</sup>) for 1.32 years (ship time), then stopping its engines and coasting for the next 17.3 years (ship time) at a constant speed, then decelerating again for 1.32 ship-years, and coming to a stop at the destination. After a short visit, the astronaut could return to Earth the same way. After the full round-trip, the clocks on board the ship show that 40 years have passed, but according to those on Earth, the ship comes back 76 years after launch.\n</p><p>From the viewpoint of the astronaut, onboard clocks seem to be running normally. The star ahead seems to be approaching at a speed of 0.87 light years per ship-year. The universe would appear contracted along the direction of travel to half the size it had when the ship was at rest; the distance between that star and the Sun would seem to be 16 light years as measured by the astronaut.\n</p><p>At higher speeds, the time on board will run even slower, so the astronaut could travel to the center of the <a href="/wiki/Milky_Way" title="Milky Way">Milky Way</a> (30,000 light years from Earth) and back in 40 years ship-time. But the speed according to Earth clocks will always be less than 1 light year per Earth year, so, when back home, the astronaut will find that more than 60 thousand years will have passed on Earth.\n</p>\n<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Constant_acceleration">Constant acceleration</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Interstellar_travel&amp;action=edit&amp;section=19" title="Edit section: Constant acceleration">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>\n<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/Space_travel_using_constant_acceleration" title="Space travel using constant acceleration">Space travel using constant acceleration</a></div>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:312px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Roundtriptimes.png"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="573" data-file-width="548" decoding="async" height="324" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f5/Roundtriptimes.png/310px-Roundtriptimes.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f5/Roundtriptimes.png/465px-Roundtriptimes.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/f/f5/Roundtriptimes.png 2x" width="310"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Roundtriptimes.png" title="Enlarge"></a></div>This plot shows a ship capable of 1-<a href="/wiki/Gravitational_acceleration" title="Gravitational acceleration">g</a> (10&#160;m/s<sup>2</sup> or about 1.0 ly/y<sup>2</sup>) "felt" or proper-acceleration<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-33"><a href="#cite_note-33">[33]</a></sup> can go far, except for the problem of accelerating on-board propellant.</div></div></div>\n<p>Regardless of how it is achieved, a propulsion system that could produce acceleration continuously from departure to arrival would be the fastest method of travel. A constant acceleration journey is one where the propulsion system accelerates the ship at a constant rate for the first half of the journey, and then decelerates for the second half, so that it arrives at the destination stationary relative to where it began.  If this were performed with an acceleration similar to that experienced at the Earth\'s surface, it would have the added advantage of producing artificial "gravity" for the crew. Supplying the energy required, however, would be prohibitively expensive with current technology.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-34"><a href="#cite_note-34">[34]</a></sup>\n</p><p>From the perspective of a planetary observer, the ship will appear to accelerate steadily at first, but then more gradually as it approaches the speed of light (which it cannot exceed). It will undergo <a href="/wiki/Hyperbolic_motion_(relativity)" title="Hyperbolic motion (relativity)">hyperbolic motion</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-35"><a href="#cite_note-35">[35]</a></sup> The ship will be close to the speed of light after about a year of accelerating and remain at that speed until it brakes for the end of the journey.\n</p><p>From the perspective of an onboard observer, the crew will feel a <a href="/wiki/Gravitational_field" title="Gravitational field">gravitational field</a> opposite the engine\'s acceleration, and the universe ahead will appear to fall in that field, undergoing hyperbolic motion. As part of this, distances between objects in the direction of the ship\'s motion will gradually contract until the ship begins to decelerate, at which time an onboard observer\'s experience of the gravitational field will be reversed.\n</p><p>When the ship reaches its destination, if it were to exchange a message with its origin planet, it would find that less time had elapsed on board than had elapsed for the planetary observer, due to <a href="/wiki/Time_dilation" title="Time dilation">time dilation</a> and <a href="/wiki/Length_contraction" title="Length contraction">length contraction</a>.\n</p><p>The result is an impressively fast journey for the crew.\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Propulsion">Propulsion</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Interstellar_travel&amp;action=edit&amp;section=20" title="Edit section: Propulsion">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Rocket_concepts">Rocket concepts</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Interstellar_travel&amp;action=edit&amp;section=21" title="Edit section: Rocket concepts">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<p>All rocket concepts are limited by the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Rocket_equation" title="Rocket equation">rocket equation</a>, which sets the characteristic velocity available as a function of exhaust velocity and mass ratio, the ratio of initial (<i>M</i><sub>0</sub>, including fuel) to final (<i>M</i><sub>1</sub>, fuel depleted) mass.\n</p><p>Very high <a href="/wiki/Power-to-weight_ratio" title="Power-to-weight ratio">specific power</a>, the ratio of thrust to total vehicle mass, is required to reach interstellar targets within sub-century time-frames.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-36"><a href="#cite_note-36">[36]</a></sup> Some heat transfer is inevitable and a tremendous heating load must be adequately handled.\n</p><p>Thus, for interstellar rocket concepts of all technologies, a key engineering problem (seldom explicitly discussed) is limiting the heat transfer from the exhaust stream back into the vehicle.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-37"><a href="#cite_note-37">[37]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Ion_engine">Ion engine</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Interstellar_travel&amp;action=edit&amp;section=22" title="Edit section: Ion engine">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>\n<p>A type of electric propulsion, spacecraft such as <a href="/wiki/Dawn_(spacecraft)" title="Dawn (spacecraft)">Dawn</a> use an <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ion_engine" title="Ion engine">ion engine</a>. In an ion engine, electric power is used to create charged particles of the propellant, usually the gas xenon, and accelerate them to extremely high velocities. The exhaust velocity of conventional rockets is limited by the chemical energy stored in the fuel\'s molecular bonds, which limits the thrust to about 5&#160;km/s. They produce a high thrust (about 10&#8310; N), but they have a low specific impulse, and that limits their top speed. By contrast, ion engines have low force, but the top speed in principle is limited only by the electrical power available on the spacecraft and on the gas ions being accelerated. The exhaust speed of the charged particles range from 15&#160;km/s to 35&#160;km/s.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-http://www.iflscience.com_38-0"><a href="#cite_note-http://www.iflscience.com-38">[38]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Nuclear_fission_powered">Nuclear fission powered</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Interstellar_travel&amp;action=edit&amp;section=23" title="Edit section: Nuclear fission powered">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>\n<h5><span class="mw-headline" id="Fission-electric">Fission-electric</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Interstellar_travel&amp;action=edit&amp;section=24" title="Edit section: Fission-electric">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h5>\n<p>Nuclear-electric or plasma engines, operating for long periods at low thrust and powered by fission reactors, have the potential to reach speeds much greater than chemically powered vehicles or nuclear-thermal rockets. Such vehicles probably have the potential to power solar system exploration with reasonable trip times within the current century. Because of their low-thrust propulsion, they would be limited to off-planet, deep-space operation. <a href="/wiki/Electrically_powered_spacecraft_propulsion" title="Electrically powered spacecraft propulsion">Electrically powered spacecraft propulsion</a> powered by a portable power-source, say a <a href="/wiki/Nuclear_reactor" title="Nuclear reactor">nuclear reactor</a>, producing only small accelerations, would take centuries to reach for example 15% of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Velocity_of_light" title="Velocity of light">velocity of light</a>, thus unsuitable for interstellar flight during a single human lifetime.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-scholar.googleusercontent.com_39-0"><a href="#cite_note-scholar.googleusercontent.com-39">[39]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h5><span class="mw-headline" id="Fission-fragment">Fission-fragment</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Interstellar_travel&amp;action=edit&amp;section=25" title="Edit section: Fission-fragment">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h5>\n<p><a href="/wiki/Fission-fragment_rocket" title="Fission-fragment rocket">Fission-fragment rockets</a> use <a href="/wiki/Nuclear_fission" title="Nuclear fission">nuclear fission</a> to create high-speed jets of fission fragments, which are ejected at speeds of up to 12,000&#160;km/s (7,500&#160;mi/s). With fission, the energy output is approximately 0.1% of the total mass-energy of the reactor fuel and limits the effective exhaust velocity to about 5% of the velocity of light. For maximum velocity, the reaction mass should optimally consist of fission products, the "ash" of the primary energy source, so no extra reaction mass need be bookkept in the mass ratio.\n</p>\n<h5><span class="mw-headline" id="Nuclear_pulse">Nuclear pulse</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Interstellar_travel&amp;action=edit&amp;section=26" title="Edit section: Nuclear pulse">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h5>\n<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Nuclear_pulse_propulsion" title="Nuclear pulse propulsion">Nuclear pulse propulsion</a></div>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Modern_Pulsed_Fission_Propulsion_Concept.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2392" data-file-width="3000" decoding="async" height="175" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/cf/Modern_Pulsed_Fission_Propulsion_Concept.jpg/220px-Modern_Pulsed_Fission_Propulsion_Concept.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/cf/Modern_Pulsed_Fission_Propulsion_Concept.jpg/330px-Modern_Pulsed_Fission_Propulsion_Concept.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/cf/Modern_Pulsed_Fission_Propulsion_Concept.jpg/440px-Modern_Pulsed_Fission_Propulsion_Concept.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Modern_Pulsed_Fission_Propulsion_Concept.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Modern Pulsed Fission Propulsion Concept.</div></div></div><p>Based on work in the late 1950s to the early 1960s, it has been technically possible to build spaceships with <a href="/wiki/Nuclear_pulse_propulsion" title="Nuclear pulse propulsion">nuclear pulse propulsion</a> engines, i.e. driven by a series of nuclear explosions. This propulsion system contains the prospect of very high <a href="/wiki/Specific_impulse" title="Specific impulse">specific impulse</a> (space travel\'s equivalent of fuel economy) and high <a href="/wiki/Power-to-weight_ratio" title="Power-to-weight ratio">specific power</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-GeneralDynamics1964NuclearPulseVehicleCondensed_40-0"><a href="#cite_note-GeneralDynamics1964NuclearPulseVehicleCondensed-40">[40]</a></sup>\n</p><p><a href="/wiki/Project_Orion_(nuclear_propulsion)" title="Project Orion (nuclear propulsion)">Project Orion</a> team member <a href="/wiki/Freeman_Dyson" title="Freeman Dyson">Freeman Dyson</a> proposed in 1968 an interstellar spacecraft using nuclear pulse propulsion that used pure <a href="/wiki/Deuterium_fusion" title="Deuterium fusion">deuterium fusion</a> detonations with a very high fuel-<a href="/wiki/Burnup" title="Burnup">burnup</a> fraction. He computed an exhaust velocity of 15,000&#160;km/s and a 100,000-tonne space vehicle able to achieve a 20,000&#160;km/s <a href="/wiki/Delta-v" title="Delta-v">delta-v</a> allowing a flight-time to <a href="/wiki/Alpha_Centauri" title="Alpha Centauri">Alpha Centauri</a> of 130 years.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Interstellar_Transport_41-0"><a href="#cite_note-Interstellar_Transport-41">[41]</a></sup> Later studies indicate that the top cruise velocity that can theoretically be achieved by a Teller-Ulam thermonuclear unit powered Orion starship, assuming no fuel is saved for slowing back down, is about 8% to 10% of the speed of light (0.08-0.1c).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-42"><a href="#cite_note-42">[42]</a></sup> An atomic (fission) Orion can achieve perhaps 3%-5% of the speed of light. A nuclear pulse drive starship powered by fusion-antimatter catalyzed nuclear pulse propulsion units would be similarly in the 10% range and pure matter-antimatter annihilation rockets would be theoretically capable of obtaining a velocity between 50% to 80% of the speed of light. In each case saving fuel for slowing down halves the maximum speed. The concept of using a <a href="/wiki/Magnetic_sail" title="Magnetic sail">magnetic sail</a> to decelerate the spacecraft as it approaches its destination has been discussed as an alternative to using propellant, this would allow the ship to travel near the maximum theoretical velocity.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-43"><a href="#cite_note-43">[43]</a></sup> Alternative designs utilizing similar principles include <a href="/wiki/Project_Longshot" title="Project Longshot">Project Longshot</a>, <a href="/wiki/Project_Daedalus" title="Project Daedalus">Project Daedalus</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Mini-Mag_Orion" title="Mini-Mag Orion">Mini-Mag Orion</a>. The principle of external nuclear pulse propulsion to maximize survivable power has remained common among serious concepts for interstellar flight without external power beaming and for very high-performance interplanetary flight.\n</p><p>In the 1970s the Nuclear Pulse Propulsion concept further was refined by <a href="/wiki/Project_Daedalus" title="Project Daedalus">Project Daedalus</a> by use of externally triggered <a href="/wiki/Inertial_confinement_fusion" title="Inertial confinement fusion">inertial confinement fusion</a>, in this case producing fusion explosions via compressing fusion fuel pellets with high-powered electron beams. Since then, <a href="/wiki/Laser" title="Laser">lasers</a>, <a href="/wiki/Ion_beam" title="Ion beam">ion beams</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Neutral_particle_beam" title="Neutral particle beam">neutral particle beams</a> and hyper-kinetic projectiles have been suggested to produce nuclear pulses for propulsion purposes.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-The_Release_of_Thermonuclear_Energy_by_Inertial_Confinement_44-0"><a href="#cite_note-The_Release_of_Thermonuclear_Energy_by_Inertial_Confinement-44">[44]</a></sup>\n</p><p>A current impediment to the development of <i>any</i> nuclear-explosion-powered spacecraft is the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Partial_Test_Ban_Treaty" title="Partial Test Ban Treaty">1963 Partial Test Ban Treaty</a>, which includes a prohibition on the detonation of any nuclear devices (even non-weapon based) in outer space. This treaty would, therefore, need to be renegotiated, although a project on the scale of an interstellar mission using currently foreseeable technology would probably require international cooperation on at least the scale of the <a href="/wiki/International_Space_Station" title="International Space Station">International Space Station</a>.\n</p><p>Another issue to be considered, would be the <a href="/wiki/G-force" title="G-force">g-forces</a> imparted to a rapidly accelerated spacecraft, cargo, and passengers inside (see <a href="/wiki/Inertia_negation" title="Inertia negation">Inertia negation</a>).\n</p>\n<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Nuclear_fusion_rockets">Nuclear fusion rockets</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Interstellar_travel&amp;action=edit&amp;section=27" title="Edit section: Nuclear fusion rockets">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>\n<p><a href="/wiki/Fusion_rocket" title="Fusion rocket">Fusion rocket</a> starships, powered by <a href="/wiki/Nuclear_fusion" title="Nuclear fusion">nuclear fusion</a> reactions, should conceivably be able to reach speeds of the order of 10% of that of light, based on energy considerations alone. In theory, a large number of stages could push a vehicle arbitrarily close to the speed of light.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-L.D._Jaffe_1963,_pp._49-58_45-0"><a href="#cite_note-L.D._Jaffe_1963,_pp._49-58-45">[45]</a></sup> These would "burn" such light element fuels as deuterium, tritium, <sup>3</sup>He, <sup>11</sup>B, and <sup>7</sup>Li. Because fusion yields about 0.3&#8211;0.9% of the mass of the nuclear fuel as released energy, it is energetically more favorable than fission, which releases &lt;0.1% of the fuel\'s mass-energy. The maximum exhaust velocities potentially energetically available are correspondingly higher than for fission, typically 4&#8211;10% of c. However, the most easily achievable fusion reactions release a large fraction of their energy as high-energy neutrons, which are a significant source of energy loss. Thus, although these concepts seem to offer the best (nearest-term) prospects for travel to the nearest stars within a (long) human lifetime, they still involve massive technological and engineering difficulties, which may turn out to be intractable for decades or centuries.\n</p>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Daedalus_ship.png"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="474" data-file-width="555" decoding="async" height="188" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b7/Daedalus_ship.png/220px-Daedalus_ship.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b7/Daedalus_ship.png/330px-Daedalus_ship.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b7/Daedalus_ship.png/440px-Daedalus_ship.png 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Daedalus_ship.png" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Daedalus interstellar vehicle.</div></div></div><p>Early studies include <a href="/wiki/Project_Daedalus" title="Project Daedalus">Project Daedalus</a>, performed by the <a href="/wiki/British_Interplanetary_Society" title="British Interplanetary Society">British Interplanetary Society</a> in 1973&#8211;1978, and <a href="/wiki/Project_Longshot" title="Project Longshot">Project Longshot</a>, a student project sponsored by <a href="/wiki/NASA" title="NASA">NASA</a> and the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/US_Naval_Academy" title="US Naval Academy">US Naval Academy</a>, completed in 1988. Another fairly detailed vehicle system, "Discovery II",<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-46"><a href="#cite_note-46">[46]</a></sup> designed and optimized for crewed Solar System exploration, based on the D<sup>3</sup>He reaction but using hydrogen as reaction mass, has been described by a team from NASA\'s <a href="/wiki/Glenn_Research_Center" title="Glenn Research Center">Glenn Research Center</a>. It achieves characteristic velocities of &gt;300&#160;km/s with an acceleration of ~1.7&#8226;10<sup>&#8722;3</sup> <i>g</i>, with a ship initial mass of ~1700 metric tons, and payload fraction above 10%. Although these are still far short of the requirements for interstellar travel on human timescales, the study seems to represent a reasonable benchmark towards what may be approachable within several decades, which is not impossibly beyond the current state-of-the-art. Based on the concept\'s 2.2% <a href="/wiki/Burnup" title="Burnup">burnup</a> fraction it could achieve a pure fusion product exhaust velocity of ~3,000&#160;km/s.\n</p><h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Antimatter_rockets">Antimatter rockets</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Interstellar_travel&amp;action=edit&amp;section=28" title="Edit section: Antimatter rockets">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>\n<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Antimatter_rocket" title="Antimatter rocket">Antimatter rocket</a></div>\n<table class="box-Refimprove_section plainlinks metadata ambox ambox-content ambox-Refimprove" role="presentation"><tbody><tr><td class="mbox-image"><div style="width:52px"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Question_book-new.svg"><img alt="" data-file-height="399" data-file-width="512" decoding="async" height="39" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/9/99/Question_book-new.svg/50px-Question_book-new.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/9/99/Question_book-new.svg/75px-Question_book-new.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/9/99/Question_book-new.svg/100px-Question_book-new.svg.png 2x" width="50"/></a></div></td><td class="mbox-text"><div class="mbox-text-span">This section <b>needs additional citations for <a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Verifiability" title="Wikipedia:Verifiability">verification</a></b>.<span class="hide-when-compact"> Please help <a class="external text" href="//en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Interstellar_travel&amp;action=edit">improve this article</a> by <a href="/wiki/Help:Introduction_to_referencing_with_Wiki_Markup/1" title="Help:Introduction to referencing with Wiki Markup/1">adding citations to reliable sources</a>. Unsourced material may be challenged and removed.<br/><small><span class="plainlinks"><i>Find sources:</i>&#160;<a class="external text" href="//www.google.com/search?as_eq=wikipedia&amp;q=%22Interstellar+travel%22" rel="nofollow">"Interstellar travel"</a>&#160;&#8211;&#160;<a class="external text" href="//www.google.com/search?tbm=nws&amp;q=%22Interstellar+travel%22+-wikipedia" rel="nofollow">news</a>&#160;<b>&#183;</b> <a class="external text" href="//www.google.com/search?&amp;q=%22Interstellar+travel%22+site:news.google.com/newspapers&amp;source=newspapers" rel="nofollow">newspapers</a>&#160;<b>&#183;</b> <a class="external text" href="//www.google.com/search?tbs=bks:1&amp;q=%22Interstellar+travel%22+-wikipedia" rel="nofollow">books</a>&#160;<b>&#183;</b> <a class="external text" href="//scholar.google.com/scholar?q=%22Interstellar+travel%22" rel="nofollow">scholar</a>&#160;<b>&#183;</b> <a class="external text" href="https://www.jstor.org/action/doBasicSearch?Query=%22Interstellar+travel%22&amp;acc=on&amp;wc=on" rel="nofollow">JSTOR</a></span></small></span> <small class="date-container"><i>(<span class="date">August 2015</span>)</i></small><small class="hide-when-compact"><i> (<a href="/wiki/Help:Maintenance_template_removal" title="Help:Maintenance template removal">Learn how and when to remove this template message</a>)</i></small></div></td></tr></tbody></table>\n<p>An <a href="/wiki/Antimatter_rocket" title="Antimatter rocket">antimatter rocket</a> would have a far higher energy density and specific impulse than any other proposed class of rocket.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-crawist_31-1"><a href="#cite_note-crawist-31">[31]</a></sup> If energy resources and efficient production methods are found to make <a href="/wiki/Antimatter" title="Antimatter">antimatter</a> in the quantities required and store<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-47"><a href="#cite_note-47">[47]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-48"><a href="#cite_note-48">[48]</a></sup> it safely, it would be theoretically possible to reach speeds of several tens of percent that of light.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-crawist_31-2"><a href="#cite_note-crawist-31">[31]</a></sup> Whether antimatter propulsion could lead to the higher speeds (&gt;90% that of light) at which relativistic <a href="/wiki/Time_dilation" title="Time dilation">time dilation</a> would become more noticeable, thus making time pass at a slower rate for the travelers as perceived by an outside observer, is doubtful owing to the large quantity of antimatter that would be required.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-crawist_31-3"><a href="#cite_note-crawist-31">[31]</a></sup>\n</p><p>Speculating that production and storage of antimatter should become feasible, two further issues need to be considered. First, in the annihilation of antimatter, much of the energy is lost as high-energy <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Gamma_radiation" title="Gamma radiation">gamma radiation</a>, and especially also as <a href="/wiki/Neutrino" title="Neutrino">neutrinos</a>, so that only about 40% of <i>mc</i><sup>2</sup> would actually be available if the antimatter were simply allowed to annihilate into radiations thermally.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-crawist_31-4"><a href="#cite_note-crawist-31">[31]</a></sup> Even so, the energy available for propulsion would be substantially higher than the ~1% of <i>mc</i><sup>2</sup> yield of nuclear fusion, the next-best rival candidate.\n</p><p>Second, heat transfer from the exhaust to the vehicle seems likely to transfer enormous wasted energy into the ship (e.g. for 0.1<i>g</i> ship acceleration, approaching 0.3 trillion watts per ton of ship mass), considering the large fraction of the energy that goes into penetrating gamma rays. Even assuming shielding was provided to protect the payload (and passengers on a crewed vehicle), some of the energy would inevitably heat the vehicle, and may thereby prove a limiting factor if useful accelerations are to be achieved.\n</p><p>More recently, <a href="/wiki/Friedwardt_Winterberg" title="Friedwardt Winterberg">Friedwardt Winterberg</a> proposed that a matter-antimatter GeV gamma ray laser photon rocket is possible by a relativistic proton-antiproton pinch discharge, where the recoil from the laser beam is transmitted by the <a href="/wiki/M%C3%B6ssbauer_effect" title="M&#246;ssbauer effect">M&#246;ssbauer effect</a> to the spacecraft.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-AA-20120821_49-0"><a href="#cite_note-AA-20120821-49">[49]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Rockets_with_an_external_energy_source">Rockets with an external energy source</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Interstellar_travel&amp;action=edit&amp;section=29" title="Edit section: Rockets with an external energy source">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>\n<p>Rockets deriving their power from external sources, such as a <a href="/wiki/Laser" title="Laser">laser</a>, could replace their internal energy source with an energy collector, potentially reducing the mass of the ship greatly and allowing much higher travel speeds. <a href="/wiki/Geoffrey_A._Landis" title="Geoffrey A. Landis">Geoffrey A. Landis</a> has proposed for an <a href="/wiki/Interstellar_probe" title="Interstellar probe">interstellar probe</a>, with energy supplied by an external laser from a base station powering an <a href="/wiki/Ion_thruster" title="Ion thruster">Ion thruster</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-geoffreylandis.com_50-0"><a href="#cite_note-geoffreylandis.com-50">[50]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Non-rocket_concepts">Non-rocket concepts</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Interstellar_travel&amp;action=edit&amp;section=30" title="Edit section: Non-rocket concepts">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<p>A problem with all traditional rocket propulsion methods is that the spacecraft would need to carry its fuel with it, thus making it very massive, in accordance with the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Rocket_equation" title="Rocket equation">rocket equation</a>. Several concepts attempt to escape from this problem:<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-crawist_31-5"><a href="#cite_note-crawist-31">[31]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-51"><a href="#cite_note-51">[51]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Interstellar_ramjets">Interstellar ramjets</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Interstellar_travel&amp;action=edit&amp;section=31" title="Edit section: Interstellar ramjets">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>\n<p>In 1960, <a href="/wiki/Robert_W._Bussard" title="Robert W. Bussard">Robert W. Bussard</a> proposed the <a href="/wiki/Bussard_ramjet" title="Bussard ramjet">Bussard ramjet</a>, a fusion rocket in which a huge scoop would collect the diffuse hydrogen in interstellar space, "burn" it on the fly using a <a href="/wiki/Proton%E2%80%93proton_chain_reaction" title="Proton&#8211;proton chain reaction">proton&#8211;proton chain reaction</a>, and expel it out of the back. Later calculations with more accurate estimates suggest that the thrust generated would be less than the drag caused by any conceivable scoop design.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (May 2016)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup> Yet the idea is attractive because the fuel would be collected <i>en route</i> (commensurate with the concept of <i>energy harvesting</i>), so the craft could theoretically accelerate to near the speed of light. The limitation is due to the fact that the reaction can only accelerate the propellant to 0.12c. Thus the drag of catching interstellar dust and the thrust of accelerating that same dust to 0.12c would be the same when the speed is 0.12c, preventing further acceleration.\n</p>\n<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Beamed_propulsion">Beamed propulsion</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Interstellar_travel&amp;action=edit&amp;section=32" title="Edit section: Beamed propulsion">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Forward-sailcraft-scheme.png"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="180" data-file-width="460" decoding="async" height="86" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/67/Forward-sailcraft-scheme.png/220px-Forward-sailcraft-scheme.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/67/Forward-sailcraft-scheme.png/330px-Forward-sailcraft-scheme.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/67/Forward-sailcraft-scheme.png/440px-Forward-sailcraft-scheme.png 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Forward-sailcraft-scheme.png" title="Enlarge"></a></div>This diagram illustrates <a href="/wiki/Robert_L._Forward" title="Robert L. Forward">Robert L. Forward</a>\'s scheme for slowing down an interstellar <a href="/wiki/Solar_sail" title="Solar sail">light-sail</a> at the star system destination.</div></div></div>\n<p>A <a href="/wiki/Solar_sail" title="Solar sail">light sail</a> or <a href="/wiki/Magnetic_sail" title="Magnetic sail">magnetic sail</a> powered by a massive <a href="/wiki/Laser" title="Laser">laser</a> or particle accelerator in the home star system could potentially reach even greater speeds than rocket- or pulse propulsion methods, because it would not need to carry its own <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Reaction_mass" title="Reaction mass">reaction mass</a> and therefore would only need to accelerate the craft\'s <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Payload_(air_and_space_craft)" title="Payload (air and space craft)">payload</a>. <a href="/wiki/Robert_L._Forward" title="Robert L. Forward">Robert L. Forward</a> proposed a means for decelerating an interstellar light sail in the destination star system without requiring a laser array to be present in that system. In this scheme, a smaller secondary sail is deployed to the rear of the spacecraft, whereas the large primary sail is detached from the craft to keep moving forward on its own. Light is reflected from the large primary sail to the secondary sail, which is used to decelerate the secondary sail and the spacecraft payload.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Forward1984RoundtripInterstellar_52-0"><a href="#cite_note-Forward1984RoundtripInterstellar-52">[52]</a></sup> In 2002, <a href="/wiki/Geoffrey_A._Landis" title="Geoffrey A. Landis">Geoffrey A. Landis</a> of <a href="/wiki/NASA" title="NASA">NASA</a>\'s Glen Research center also proposed a laser-powered, propulsion, sail ship that would host a diamond sail (of a few nanometers thick) powered with the use of <a href="/wiki/Solar_energy" title="Solar energy">solar energy</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-53"><a href="#cite_note-53">[53]</a></sup> With this proposal, this interstellar ship would, theoretically, be able to reach 10 percent the speed of light.\n</p><p>A <a href="/wiki/Magnetic_sail" title="Magnetic sail">magnetic sail</a> could also decelerate at its destination without depending on carried fuel or a driving beam in the destination system, by interacting with the plasma found in the solar wind of the destination star and the interstellar medium.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-54"><a href="#cite_note-54">[54]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-55"><a href="#cite_note-55">[55]</a></sup>\n</p><p>The following table lists some example concepts using beamed laser propulsion as proposed by the physicist <a href="/wiki/Robert_L._Forward" title="Robert L. Forward">Robert L. Forward</a>:<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-56"><a href="#cite_note-56">[56]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<table class="wikitable">\n<tbody><tr>\n<th>Mission</th>\n<th>Laser Power</th>\n<th>Vehicle Mass</th>\n<th>Acceleration</th>\n<th>Sail Diameter</th>\n<th>Maximum Velocity <br/> (% of the speed of light)\n</th></tr>\n<tr>\n<td colspan="6">1. Rendezvous &#8211; Alpha Centauri, 41 years\n</td></tr>\n<tr>\n<td><i>outbound stage</i></td>\n<td>7,200 GW</td>\n<td>785 t</td>\n<td>0.005 g</td>\n<td>100&#160;km</td>\n<td>21% @ 4.29 ly<sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Accuracy_dispute#Disputed_statement" title="Wikipedia:Accuracy dispute"><span title="The material near this tag is possibly inaccurate or nonfactual.
